The laterals in el [jelɣ] ‘ate’ and dolg [dolɣk] ‘duty’ are clearly velarised (Jones and Ward 1969: 109-110) as are the nasals, cf. nužnyi [nɣuʒnɣɨj] ‘necessary’ where both nasals are velarised. In both Russian and Irish the primary articulation is still dental; velarisation is achieved by lowering the body of the tongue while simultaneously raising the back.

In Irish, as in Russian, both /r/ and /rj/ exist but the realisation of them is different. Both of them are apical but in Irish /r/ is either a frictionless continuant, like English [ɹ], but velarised, when it occurs initially, medially (intervocalically) or word-finally after a vowel, e.g. rua [ɹuə] ‘red-haired’, árasán [ɑ:ɹəsɑ:nɣ] ‘flat, apartment’, cur [kʌɹ] ‘putting’, or it is a flap when it comes after a stop and before a stressed vowel brón [bɾo:nɣ] ‘sorrow’. /r/ is not rolled in Western Irish (unless for emphasis) although in the North a rolled [r] occurs as the articulation of /r/ with some older speakers; this has generally been replaced by a non-trill realisation.

The only similarity between Irish and Russian here is the tap articulation, which in Russian may occur when /r/ is found intervocalically and does not immediately precede a stressed vowel, e.g. gorod [ˡgoɾət] ‘town’, xorošo [xəɾʌˡʃo] ‘good’. Otherwise a slight trill is the normal realisation of non-palatal /r/.

The palatal /rj/ of Russian maintains its trill character with simultaneous raising of the tongue body towards the palate. This raising of the tongue is accompanied by closing of the jaws and slight lip spreading (Bolla 1981: 99-100, plate 77). On the whole Russian shows greater consistency in the realisation of /r/ and /rj/. In Irish palatal /rj/ is realised with considerable tongue body raising and is never a flap or trill. It is found at its clearest in intervocalic or pre-stress, post-stop position: amáireach [əˡmɑːɹjəx]213 ‘tomorrow’, tríd [tjɹji:dj] ‘between’.

The functional load of the /r/ # /rj/ contrast in pre-vocalic initial position is slight in Irish. The few instances of this which exist tend to neutralise the distinction in favour of /r/: reangach [ɹæŋgəx] ‘wiry, sinewy’, reatha [ɹæə] ‘run.GEN’. The palatal allophone [æ] of the low vowel /a/ remains as an indicator of the phonological palatality of the preceding [ɹ]. A similarity to this situation is found in Belarusian which, like Russian itself, has a palatal # non-palatal series of consonants but has lost /rj/ (Birillo et al. 1966: 163).

Furthermore there is no evidence in Irish for the alternative of sequentialising the apico-alveolar and palatal articulations so that the sequence /r/+/j/ does not occur nor are there any cases of palatal /rj/ plus /j/ as in Russian r’janyj /rjjanij/ ‘zealous’.





5. 4. 6. Labialisation of vowels


In Russian an additional factor plays a role in the realisation of non-palatal consonants. Velarised consonants, when they occur before high or mid back vowels, i.e. /u/, /o/ or /i/ (= [ɨ]) are also labialised in this position (Jones and Ward 1969: 79). In Irish this labialisation is slight as consonants and back vowels do not have the labial to non-labial transition during their articulation which is characteristic of Russian and most evident with /o/, e.g. noskij [n’oskjij] ‘durable (of clothes)’. Velarisation of nasals in Russian is clearest before /i/ ([ɨ]), /u/, /o/ and to a certain extent before /a/, e.g. nam [nɣam] ‘us. DATIVE’, ona [ʌˡnɣa] ‘she’ (when stressed). As [nɣ] does not occur before [i] (this being the allophone of /i/ after a palatal consonant) the issue does not arise nor does it with /e/ as non-palatal nasals (or other consonants, Jones and Ward 1969: 198) cannot precede the mid front vowel. This statement must be qualified somewhat to include the position of loanwords and loan-morphemes. Thus in Russian examples of non-palatals before the mid-vowel /e/ are found with loanwords such as tennis [ˡtenjis] ‘tennis’.

In Irish non-palatals are found before front vowels, irrespective of the origin of the words in question. They are correspondingly velarised to optimise the non-palatal # palatal distinction: Gaeilge [gɣe:ljgjə] ‘Irish’, naoi [nɣi:] ‘nine’, néal [nje:lɣ] ‘cloud’.





5. 4, 7. Inflection in Irish and Russian


The phonological parallels between Irish and Russian are not matched by similarities in the morphology of both languages. In the case of Russian one has variable stress, frequently falling on inflections and thus retaining them with their full phonetic value. Irish has strong initial stress and inflections have become increasingly indistinct in the course of the language’s history. The weight of exponence has been shifted to consonants with the opposition palatal # non-palatal. This system is supported by the completeness and regularity of the opposition in Irish phonology and by the fact that it does not involve further changes such as the assibilation characteristic of the Slavic palatalisations (Carlton 1990: 97). As inflections are never stressed there is no possibility of an alternation between stem vowel and zero which one has quite frequently in Russian as with so-called unstable vowels in oblique cases of nouns (Pulkina 1981: 25-26), e.g. rot ~ rta ‘mouth’, lev ~ l’va ‘lion’.





IV Typological viewpoints




1. Introduction


This section is concerned with comparing initial mutation and similar sandhi phenomena in a variety of languages to the situation in Irish with a view to reaching valid typological generalisations concerning mutation systems within the phonologies of documented languages (Maddieson 2011; Vilupillai 2012: 61-87). The three main Celtic languages, apart from Irish, i.e. Scottish Gaelic, Welsh and Breton, are considered because all the

Celtic languages exhibit initial mutation, due to their common source, 214 with individual variations in the modern languages. Then a number of other languages and dialects, from both within Europe and beyond, are examined to see how mutation-like phenomena manifest themselves. Some languages have morphonologically relevant mutations, e.g. Atlantic-Congo languages, like Fula and Serer, and the Paleosiberian language isolate Nivkh, while others have low-level phenomena which represent a phonetic framework similar to mutation but which has not developed into a system indicating grammatical categories. Instances here would be Tuscan Italian and Canary Spanish.





2. Scottish Gaelic


The two mutations of Irish, lenition and nasalisation, along with zero mutation, exist in Scottish Gaelic with similar morphonological functions to Irish. The shifts in articulation, which are characteristic of Irish, are also found, implying that the mutations were taken more or less in their present form to Scotland by Irish speakers who settled there in the first millennium CE. If this is not true then at least the innovations in Irish in the Middle Irish period (900-1200), which include the shift of D to ɣ on lenition, must have spread to Scotland. There are, however, changes which took place in Scotland and which render the picture of the mutations in Irish and Scottish Gaelic different. The major change is the shift among stops: the voiceless stops are very strongly aspirated and the voiced ones have become unaspirated voiceless stops, e.g. Irish /p/ = Scottish Gaelic /ph/, Irish /b/ = Scottish Gaelic /p/. The changes which lenition entails in Scottish Gaelic are as follows (see Ternes 2006 [1973]: 57 based on Applecross, Ross-shire).

(1) base form lenited form

ph f

th h

kh x

f Ø

s h

p v

t ɣ

k ɣ

m v





The same correspondences can be assumed for other dialects as well, e.g. that on the large island of Lewis (Borgstrøm 1940a), which is furthest removed from Irish geographically speaking. The restrictions on lenition which apply in Irish are also found in Scottish Gaelic (Borgstrøm 1940a: 78-81), e.g. there is no lenition of S plus voiceless stop or M.215

Nasalisation in Scottish Gaelic is largely realised as phonetic prenasalisation with assimilation of place by the nasal to the following stop or fricative, /f/ (Rogers 1972).

(2) base form nasalised form

p mp

t nt

k ŋk

f ɱf

b mb

d nd

g ŋg





This is different from Irish nasalisation which is a two step process voicing input voiceless stops and nasalising voiced ones. The pre-nasalisation in Scottish Gaelic is not a recent development and is attested in early attempts to devise a specific orthography (Gillies 1993: 168). Some of the dialects, e.g. that of Lewis, have a variant of the above system in which the nasal absorbs the following stop. The resulting system is similar to that of Welsh with a series of voiceless nasals.

(3) base form nasalised form

p, f

t

k

b m

d n

g ŋ





It is difficult to determine whether currently attested forms of nasalisation always applied in this manner in Scottish Gaelic. Certain fixed forms would suggest that the Irish pattern of nasalisation once obtained and that the Scottish one was thus a later independent development, cf. /v/ bh as the nasalised form of /f/ in locative adverbs like abhán ‘down’ abhos ‘over here’.





3. Welsh


The Welsh system of initial mutation (King 1993: 15) is in principle similar to that in Irish but its manifestation is different. Three mutations are recognised and labelled as (i) the soft mutation (treiglad meddal), (ii) the spirant mutation (treiglad llaes), (iii) the nasal mutation (treiglad trwynol). The most comprehensive study of the mutations of Welsh is Ball and Müller (1990). Ball (1986a, 1986b), Thomas (1966), Awbery (1984, 1986a, 1986b, 2009) and Ewen (1982) are other significant publications in this field.

Essentially, initial mutation in Welsh is more regular because its operation involves just one change and not the shifts in place of articulation which are found in Irish. There is one more mutation in Welsh because lenition can be either fricativisation or voicing, the latter being part of nasalisation in Irish. The following is the inventory of sound segments for Welsh (see Hannahs 2013a: 14-21). As sounds do not pair as palatal and non-palatal series a simple notation is sufficient.

(4)





There are certain obvious differences in this inventory when compared to Irish. Ambidental fricatives exist and represent the output of mutating post-dental stops and are also present in the lexical forms of words in non-initial position. In Irish the situation is less regular as ambidental fricatives have long ceased to exist (they were lost in the Middle Irish period). There is no voiced velar fricative in Welsh (as opposed to Irish), this having disappeared in the history of the language.

But as opposed to Irish the mutation of the sound which would be the corresponding independent segment to /ɣ/, namely /g/, does not result in a different segment but in zero. Welsh has a gap here whereas Irish experienced a shift of place of articulation for the dependent phonemes /θ/ and /ð/, which no longer exist.

(5)





On a systemic level Welsh has voiceless sonorants (G. Jones 1984: 51). However, the phonetic realisation of these is often as a sequence of sonorant + /h/, see the discussion in Ball (1984: 6-8).216 This fact does not invalidate the phonological distinction in Welsh between a voiced series and a non-voiced series of sonorants.

Five non-voiced sonorants are listed below though not all of them are recognised on this level.217 The following list includes both independent and dependent218 segments, i.e. those which occur in the lexical, citation forms of words and those which result from initial mutation. The voiceless lateral is an independent segment, occurring word-initially, e.g. lladd [að] ‘to kill’.

The group on the left includes plosives, which show the greatest phonotactic range, but also segments, such as [v, θ, ð, x], which word-initially are confined to loanwords (as are the affricates [tʃ] and [dʒ], G. Jones 1984: 44) and a small number of native words. Medially or finally there is a wider occurrence of segments, e.g. /θ/ cath /ka:θ/ ‘cat’, /ð/ addo /aˡðo/ ‘to pledge, vow’, /ŋ/ dangos /daŋgos/ ‘to show’.

The group on the right consists of segments – voiceless nasals and the voiced velar nasal – which are only dependent, i.e. the result of mutation.

(6)219, 220, 221





The occurrence of voiced fricatives and the velar nasal is similar to that in Irish where V occurs medially while ŋ is also found medially but only as a realisation of N before G or K and not without a following velar stop.

(7)





This word-internal occurrence of these segments does not affect their status as exclusively dependent phonemes in both Welsh and Irish (see section II. 1.4 above).





3.1.1. Nasalisation


The mutation system of Welsh can be viewed as consisting of two basic mutations, lenition and nasalisation.222 The phonological directive of mutation in Welsh (and Irish) is either to weaken a segment phonetically or to nasalise it.

The manifestation of nasalisation in Welsh is simpler than in Irish. Welsh has phonologically voiceless sonorants and so nasalisation, which is the alteration of only one feature, involves the nasalisation of all inputs to the mutation.

(8) [-nasal] → [+nasal]

p

t

k

b m

d n

g ŋ





This view of nasalisation implies that one does not recognise, on a systemic level, the separate existence of /h/ post-consonantally. While it is true that Welsh has /h/ prevocalically, e.g. haul /haɨl/ ‘sun’, some analyses also posit a postconsonantal /h/ (Thomas 1966: 116-117). A frequently cited item of evidence is that, phonetically, the nasalisation of voiceless stops often results in a sequence of nasal and following [h], e.g. /p/ + nasalisation → [mh]. As voiceless stops are phonetically aspirated, this interpretation might suggest interpreting the shift as /ph/ → /mh/ where nasalisation would only refer to the stop portion of the input cluster (or in the case of voiced stops to the entire input, e.g. /b/ → /m/). But this analysis could run into difficulties in other areas where mutation operates. Consider the ‘soft mutation’, which changes voiceless stops into voiced ones, e.g. [ph] to [b]. If [ph] is to be interpreted systemically as /ph/ then ‘soft-mutation’ becomes a more complicated, two step process of (1) aspiration deletion (/ph/ → /p/) and (2) voicing (/p/ → /b/).





3.1.2. Feature interpretation of mutation


The changes in feature values on mutation are similar in Irish and Welsh when the mutation systems are viewed in their entirety. For example, a restriction on mutation in both languages is that the value of only one feature can be changed. The shifts of articulation on lenition of certain segments in Irish are not exceptions within a feature interpretation as the features which are involved in mutation in either Welsh or Irish are only [continuant], [voice] and [nasal]. The restriction on change of value does not refer to other features, for example the articulatory shift of Irish D to ɣ on lenition.

(9) Irish





Welsh





For both Irish and Welsh one must distinguish between phonetic alteration of segments and the grammatical domain of a mutation. On the latter one can see that mutations are associated with definite grammatical categories. Furthermore, a group of morphonological elements may be associated with a single mutation, e.g. plural possessive pronouns with nasalisation in Irish. The phonetic alteration of segments includes, in the two languages, changes in the value of each of the three features listed above which can be assigned to the following mutations in both languages.

(10)





The two types of lenition involve the following segments in Welsh.

(11)





Certain asymmetries can be recognised here. Firstly, /s/ and /ʃ/ do not undergo voicing although they are independent phonemes, i.e. they are found initially in lexical citation forms. Secondly, /g(w)/ cannot be phonetically fricativised; phonologically it alternates with zero. Awbery (1975: 17) gets around the alternation of /g/ with zero by stating that there is a segment /ɣ/ which has no overt phonetic form. Seen as a statement on the diachronic development of Welsh this is true but not with regard to the synchronic phonology of the language (as it would be an instance of absolute neutralisation).





3.1.3. The two leniting mutations of Welsh


The phonetic changes in the two leniting mutations of Welsh,223 fricativisation and voicing (the ‘spirant mutation’ and the ‘soft mutation’ respectively),224 are grouped according to morphonological factors. For instance, the soft mutation applies after the masculine possessive pronoun ‘his’ and the spirant mutation after the equivalent feminine pronoun ‘her’. This is true of many other grammatical categories, say the second person singular possessive which takes the soft mutation.

(12)





If one considers the series of sounds, grouped in the two leniting mutations, in purely phonetic terms then the changes in (f – h) below are cases of fricativisation but are not subsumed under the ‘spirant mutation’.

(13) Soft mutation





Spirant mutation





Prefixing of/h/

Similarly to Irish, Welsh uses a prefix h before vowel-initial words under certain grammatical conditions, for instance after the feminine possessive pronoun: Irish a harm /ə harm/ ‘her weapon’, Welsh ei hafal /i haval/ ‘her apple’. In Welsh the prefixation of /h/ is part of the spirant mutation. A word with a mutable initial consonant occurring in the same environment has this fricativised (Thomas 1966: 97-98): ei chath /i xa:θ/ ‘her cat’. This fricativisation is what is found in Irish after the masculine possessive pronoun while in Welsh voicing of voiceless stops and fricativisation of voiced ones occurs (the ‘soft mutation’, see (13) above): Irish a cheart /ə xjart/ ‘his right’, Welsh ei gath /i ga:θ/ ‘his cat’. A consequence of this for phonological analysis is that, while in Irish /h/ can be viewed as connected to the lack of mutation, in Welsh it must be taken as part of the mutation which fricativises voiceless stops (‘spirant mutation’).

Mixed mutation





In some cases where a grammatical category demands mutation there can be a mixture of mutations to avoid possible ambiguity. For instance, verbs in the negative have soft mutation of their initial consonant but only if this is one of this set: /b, d, g, , /. If the initial consonant is a voiceless stop, i.e. /p, t, k/, then the spirant mutation applies.

(14)





Possible motivation for two mutations being used to indicate a single grammatical category (a situation unknown in Irish) would be disambiguation, or at least the introduction of redundant marking as a communicative strategy. Recall from (13) above that some outputs of the soft mutation are identical with some inputs, e.g. /d/ is the output of leniting /t/ but also the input for the output /ð/, see (13b and h). By using the spirant mutation with verb stems which have an initial voiceless stop the output cannot be identical with any input form to a mutation.225





3.1.4. Major and minor lenition


Surveying the number of grammatical categories which rely for their indication on the soft mutation (see S. Williams 1980: 174) and comparing these with those which use spirant mutation (S. Williams loc. cit.) then it is obvious that the soft mutation is by far the more frequent; it is also the more common in colloquial Welsh (King 1993: 16). Both mutations in their turn have a larger scope than nasalisation, something which is also true of lenition vis à vis nasalisation in Irish.

The grammatical significance of soft mutation for Welsh would be reason enough for regarding it as the major type of lenition and spirant mutation as the minor type. But the phonology of these mutations offers additional justification for the soft mutation as major lenition and the spirant mutation as minor lenition. Major lenition affects the largest number of segments in the phonological inventory of Welsh and it furthermore has two types of input as opposed to one for minor lenition.

(15) Major lenition, ‘soft mutation’ [-cont, -voice] → [-cont, +voice] [-cont, +voice] → [+cont, +voice]

Minor lenition, ‘spirant mutation’ [-cont] → [+cont]





Seen synchronically, minor lenition can be regarded as a secondary development arising from the possibility of changing [-cont] to [+cont] (with concomitant [-voice]). There are obviously no cases of [-cont,+voice] to [+cont,+voice] in minor lenition as this would in itself be major lenition.

Similar argumentation can be offered for the product of minor lenition. The voiceless fricatives which result from it cannot serve as input to a further stage of this lenition as they cannot be phonetically altered any more, i.e. there is no process of voicing voiceless fricatives in Welsh, e.g. /f/ cannot mutate to /v/, /θ/ to /ð/, etc., i.e. [-voice, +cont] → [+voice, +cont] is not a step in any mutation.





3.1.5. Genesis of mutation


In both Welsh and Irish lenition is the result of diachronic developments and it might be useful to review its ontogenesis in order to understand how the present-day distributions in both languages came about. To begin with proclitics entered into a sandhi relationship with the word following them such that their final consonants assimilated to the initial consonant of the following word yielding geminates (see Jackson 1953: 634; Lewis and Pedersen 1937: 120; Thurneysen 1946: 91 on Irish; Jackson 1967a: 317 on Breton). Where the second word began with a vowel, /h/ was prefixed to it resulting from the fact that in many cases the original preceding consonant was /s/ which had been lenited to /h/ (as in synchronic lenition in present-day Irish). For Irish, gemination and prefix-h went together (Jackson 1953: 634). Because of the later decline of phonological geminates only prefix-h has remained in Irish.

In P-Celtic (Welsh and Breton) the original situation was the same with gemination and prefix-h. But in both Welsh and Breton the voiceless geminate stops changed to simple fricatives (e.g. */pp/ → /f/, see Jackson 1967a: 318 on Breton; D. S. Evans 1964: 21 on Welsh), rather than just degemination in Irish. The voiced geminates simplified without a change in manner of articulation (e.g. */gg/ → /g/); later changes are responsible for present-day irregularities, for instance /θ/ → /ð/ → /z/ in most Breton dialects (Hemon 1975: 5; Trépos 2001 [1980]: 29) or /g/ → /x/, /h/, Ø under varying circumstances in the different dialects (Jackson 1967a: 313-314).

The importance of these considerations is to show that for the synchronic description of mutation in Welsh there is a parallel between minor lenition and prefix-h. Furthermore, there is a correlation between minor lenition in Welsh and the absence of mutation in Irish (zero mutation). Consider the situation in Irish first.

In Irish, because minor lenition has not developed as a separate mutation as in Welsh but is part of a single lenition process, it is necessary in the synchronic phonology of the language to recognise an additional situation, viz. zero mutation where a certain grammatical category is characterised by not demanding any mutation. This can be seen with the third person singular feminine possessive pronoun which does not alter the form of an initial consonant. The relationship between zero mutation in Irish and minor lenition in Welsh can be shown here as the particular pronoun demands these two mutations in the respective languages: Irish: a teaghlach ‘her family’ (zero mutation), Welsh: a theulu ditto (th = /θ/ ← /t/ + minor lenition).

While minor lenition is clearly a mutation in Welsh it is also necessary to refer to the absence of mutation in Irish as a mutation in its own right. The diachronic fact that zero mutation was consonant gemination in Old Irish (Pedersen 1909-13: 87-92; Thurneysen 1946: 82-85) and lost its phonetic manifestation with the loss of distinctive length among consonants is irrelevant to present-day Irish. But there is synchronic justification for recognising zero mutation among morphonological alternations in the present-day language as the lack of mutation also indicates grammatical categories, such as the genitive with feminine nouns: an cháin ‘the tax’, (ch = /x/), méad na cánach ‘the amount of tax’, (c = /k/). In addition to this, zero mutation, although without phonetic manifestation with consonants, is realised as prefix-h before vowels in which case there is an alteration in the phonetic realisation of word forms subject to zero mutation.

The identification of zero mutation with prefix-h on a grammatical level in Irish is paralleled by a similar identification of minor lenition and prefix-h in Welsh. Not only that, but minor lenition has a direct phonetic link with prefix-h. In grammars of Welsh minor lenition is taken as only affecting voiceless stops (S. Williams 1980: 174, M. D. Jones 1976: 124) but in fact it also affects nasals by devoicing them (rightly noted by Pilch 1975: 96 and Thomas 1966: 117): ei mam /i / ‘her mother’, ei nain /i / ‘her grandmother’.

If one now regards /h/ as what it is phonetically, a period of voicelessness, then one can reinterpret prefix-h and minor lenition for sonorants as a process of devoicing. However, minor lenition is fricativisation for voiceless stops and so this mutation has two realisations.

(16) Minor lenition, ‘spirant mutation’

(i) devoicing V → V [-voice]

N [+voice] → N [-voice]





(ii) fricativisation P [-voice] → F [-voice]

(V = vowel; N = nasal; P = plosive; F = fricative)





But the fricativisation of voiceless stops can be offered a different interpretation in a synchronic description of Welsh. Assume that minor lenition is a global process of devoicing and that if the input to this mutation is already voiceless then it is fricativised; this latter assumption would give /p/ → /f/ as /p/ is already voiceless, i.e. it yields the correct output.

The validity of such a synchronic interpretation is found in the fact that its converse applies to major lenition. Consider first of all its two phonetic manifestations.

(17) Major lenition, ‘soft mutation’ (i) voicing

P [-voice] → P [+voice]

L [-voice] → L [+voice]



(ii) fricativisation

P [+voice] (+ /m/) → F [+voice]

(P = plosive; L = liquid; F = fricative)





If one assumes that major lenition is a single global process of voicing then one can account for its manifestation. Voiceless stops and voiceless sonorants are voiced on the operation of the mutation (/p, t, k, , / → /b, d, g, l, r/). Assume further that if the input to this mutation is already voiced then it is fricativised; this latter assumption would give /b/ → /v/ as /b/ is already voiced, i.e. it yields the correct output.

Both kinds of lenition in Welsh can thus be reduced to a single mirror-image process of voicing or devoicing (Wetzels and Mascaró 2001); this interpretation is the simplest synchronic phonological statement of the two mutations and renders the output with any given input predictable. The only exception to this is the alternation of /g/ with zero in major lenition.

(18) Voicing (major lenition, ‘soft mutation’)

(i)





(ii) if [+voice], then → [+cont] b, m → v

d → ð

g → Ø





Devoicing (minor lenition, ‘spirant mutation’)

(i)





(ii) if [-voice], then → [+cont] p → f

t → θ

k → x





4. Breton


Breton is probably the Celtic language with the most irregular phonology. To begin with the dialects vary greatly in both when mutation is triggered and what the manifestation of a particular mutation is. There is a shift of /ð/ to /z/ in nearly all dialects with the partial exception of the Vannes country. This dialect also has an occasional voicing of the voiceless fricatives which result from stops by the spirant mutation, e.g. original /p/ → /f/ becomes /p/ → /v/.

Breton is the only Celtic language to have a fortifying mutation, ‘provection’ (I. Press 2009). It occurs chiefly after ho ‘your.PL’ which originally had a voiceless velar fricative as word-final consonant, later a glottal fricative. This sound causes the following consonant, by assimilation, to become voiceless (Le Dû 1986: 449). This situation shows that the mutations are assimilation phenomena and not a general weakening rule for all the Celtic languages although the assimilation in environments of greater sonority follows general principles of weakening.

Furthermore, there also exists in Breton a so-called ‘mixed mutation’. This is found at the beginning with verbs. By ‘mixed’ is meant that some segments are affected differently than others. This mutation consists of lenition and provection (Le Dû 1986: 442), e.g. /b/ becomes /f/ which consists of spirantisation and devoicing at the same time: oh vlejal [fle:zəl] ‘shouting’ (from blejal, ‘to shout’); /g/ becomes /h/ in oh ouelo [o hwelo] ‘crying’ (from gouelo [gwelo] ‘to cry’). In modern literary Breton (Le Dû 2013) lenition has the following realisations.

(19) p → b

t → d

k → g

b → v

d → z

g → h

m → v





As in Irish the sonorant /m/ can be subject to lenition, the original output taken to be a nasalised bilabial fricative (symbolised as Greek µ by Jackson 1967a: 308). Equally /v/ ← /ß/ holds for Breton while /z/ arose from the assibilation of /ð/ (Jackson 1967a: 307-315). The voiceless fricative /x/ arose through the devoicing of /ɣ/ and instances of /ɣ/ also disappeared (Jackson 1967a: 309). Although both sets of changes in (19) are treated as lenition it is traditional to refer to the second as a spirant mutation (Jackson 1967a: 317).

A cluster affected by lenition in Breton is /gw/ → /w/. Its origin lies in the geminate bilabial approximant /ßß/, written W. This developed a velar element in the form of the plosive /g/. There are parallels for this type of development elsewhere, cf. Germanic loanwords in Romance such Old High German werra → French guerre, Italian guerra and in North Germanic Verschärfung (lit. ‘sharpening’) which consists of /j/ → /dj/ and /w/ → /gw/ (S. Anderson 1974: 167). By analogy this cluster sometimes appeared lenited as /hw/ where only the /g/ is subject to lenition yielding /x/ followed by the separate /w/. There is a further series of changes in Breton called new lenition (Jackson 1967a: 360) which is due to the output of the earlier spirant mutation (Hemon 1975: 5) acting as the input to intervocalic voicing lenition.

(20)226





For the third stage above /x/ voices and changes its place of articulation on lenition (Modern Breton has no /ɣ/, though it may occasionally appear intervocalically, Delaporte 1977: 18). The consonants which are potentially subject to voicing lenition remain voiceless in final position. This is because of the final devoicing which is operative in Breton (Jackson 1967a: 42). A further mutation has arisen as a result of final devoicing: provection. Originally caused by the effect of voicelessness on a voiced consonant beginning a following word, it has become a part of the morphonological system of Breton.227 Devoicing in Breton, diachronically and synchronically, is responsible for the following changes (Jackson 1967a: 325).

(21) b → p

d → t

g → k

ß, µ → f

ð → t

ɣ → x





Only the first column is found in Modern Breton. The second column represents a series of historical developments and is irregular in involving both plosivisation and devoicing. As Jackson (1967a: 332) suggests, this may be due to the sequence /-d-/ being plosivised to /-dd-/, devoiced to /-tt-/ and simplified to /-t-/. That this did not occur with /ß/ and /ɣ/ could hypothetically be due to the comparatively high frequency of /d/ in comparison with /b/ or /g/.

Breton also shows a syllable-bound complementary distribution of long vowels and short consonants, and vice versa, so that it allows only the following syllable coda structures: (i) -VCC, (ii) -VVC. There is also final consonant reduction in Breton which leads to certain clusters, notably sonorant and stop, losing their second element (Delaporte 1977: 18), though this may be retained in non-final positions.

(22)228





The mutations of Modern Breton can be summarised as follows.

(23)





5. Italian


Italian, in its history and present-day forms, illustrates lenition and lenition-like phenomena. Apart from intervocalic voicing (systemic in Northern Italian dialects, Lepschy and Lepschy 1977: 51, e.g. Latin RIPAM → Italian riva ‘shore’, ACUM → ago ‘needle’) and simplification, there are further developments (in various dialects) of new sounds which arose, for instance, due to palatalisation in the genesis of Modern Italian, cf. Latin CENTUM /k-/ → Italian cento /tʃ-/. In this context Tuscan Italian is relevant as it shows intervocalic lenition which reduces voiceless stops to voiceless fricatives, e.g. di/della casa /di/della hasa/ ‘of (the) house’.

(24) Plosive lenition in Tuscan Italian

k → h, Ø

p → φ

p → φ

t → θ





Tuscan Italian also displays canonical syllable structure, similar in principle to Norwegian and Swedish, but differing somewhat in detail. The similarity lies in the interdependence of nucleus length and coda length with Italian having long vowels in open syllables and short vowels in closed syllables. In the historical development from Latin geminates arose causing syllables to conform to canonical structure, e.g. Latin vitium ‘vice’ → vezzo /vet.tso/ ‘habit’ (Lewis and Short 1958 [1879]: 2000) shows a short vowel in the first closed syllable and a long vowel in the second open syllable.

Consonant lenition and gemination are part of the phenomenon known as gorgia toscana in Tuscan Italian (Marotta 2008). The fricativisation and gemination of word-initial segments depends on the original form of a preceding grammatical word. Thus the feminine article la causes fricativisation: la casa [la hasa], and the preposition a (< Latin ad) triggers gemination: a porta [a pporta] ‘to door’ < Latin ad portam (Lepschy and Lepschy 1988, Maiden and Parry eds, 1997).

(25) a. di Carlo [di harlo] ‘of Charles’

b. a Carlo [a kkarlo] ‘to Charles’

c. per Carlo [per karlo] ‘for Charles’





These phrase level sandhi phenomena are not confined to Tuscan Italian, for instance, the Logudoro dialects of Northern Sardinia show an alternation of voiceless stops with voiced fricatives via voiced stops: [porku] ‘pig’, [su ßorku] ‘the pig’. But Tuscan Italian is significant in having three possible distinctions, fricativisation, gemination and zero mutation. Three-way systems can often be analysed as consisting of a lenition mutation (usually fricativisation) and a fortition mutation (nasalisation or gemination), both of these contrasting with a lack of change, which can be labelled zero mutation, yielding a system as follows.

(26) Mutation system with three distinctions





Tuscan Italian is thus, in principle, similar to Irish in the number of possible mutations it shows.

Table 1. Mutation in Irish and Tuscan Italian



Irish Tuscan Italian

1) zero mutation no change no change

2) mutation 1 lenition lenition

3) mutation 2 nasalisation gemination



Of course the sandhi phenomena are not the sole indicators of grammatical categories in Tuscan Italian and so do not have the grammatical status of the mutations in the Celtic languages. But if di, a and per were to merge phonetically then the alternation of fricative, geminate and zero could then become grammatically relevant as it did in the pre-history of Irish.

The functionalisation of initial mutation implies that it has taken over from other grammatical devices which have been lost or at least de-functionalised in a language. For this to happen language learners must interpret the mutations as grammatically significant and background any other possible indicators of grammatical categories, e.g. inflections. With this reanalysis of the status of mutations a typological re-orientation would take place.





6. Spanish


Intervocalic voicing of voiceless stops or fricativisation of voiced ones is among the most common phonetic phenomena and hardly any grounds for comment. A special case is presented in those languages which (still) show a close phonetic bond between certain particles and lexical stems, say between articles and following nouns. Given such a bond, the initial segment of a noun, if preceded by a vowel-final particle, is subject to the same intervocalic voicing which one finds in word-internal position in such languages.





6.1. Andalusian Spanish


Phonetic alterations which develop word-initially do not always represent weakening. The fortition of word-initial segments, known traditionally as provection (Le Dû 1986: 441), can arise where voiced segments are rendered voiceless or fricatives are shifted to stops in a sandhi situation, for instance in Andalusian Spanish (Ternes 1977: 38).

(27)





Final /s/ disappeared in Andalusian Spanish229 via a stage as the glottal fricative /h/ (Malmberg 1962: 64-65). This loss affected both the plural form of the definite article los /lo/ and regular nouns which are now unaltered in the plural where previously they appended /s/. Before the complete loss of /s/, however, word-initial consonants were devoiced by assimilation. Thus for those nouns which have a voiced obstruent as the initial segment in their citation forms there is now a synchronically unmotivated alternation with a voiceless obstruent in the plural. In effect this is initial mutation as the grammatical category ‘plural’ is now realised by the change in the initial consonant of the noun.





6.2. Canary Spanish


It would appear that the loss of /s/ has had different effects in Spanish dialects. The Andalusian position is one where there is an alternation between voiced and voiceless fricatives. In Canary Spanish (Oftedal (1985: 103) the loss of /s/ has resulted in an alternation between voiced and voiceless obstruents with a possible alteration in manner of articulation.

(28)





The final /-s/ of the modifying pronoun not only voiced the initial segments (third and fourth examples above) but also blocked the fricativisation (second and fourth examples above). This means that forms with an initial /b, d, g/ now overlap with singular forms where there is intervocalic voicing of voiceless stops due to the same type of sandhi phenomenon (Oftedal 1985: 103).

(29) a. su paño, sus baños [su ˡbaɲo]

‘his cloth, his bathrooms’

b. su cherva, sus hierbas [su ˡɟerba]

‘his castor-oil plant, his herbs’





6.3. Castilian Spanish


The possibilities of phonetic lenition and the restrictions placed on it by the distribution of systemic segments is well illustrated by Castilian Spanish. 230 The first stage of lenition is phonetically regular.

(30)





The first irregularity can be seen with /g/ which has a voiceless allophone before front vowels /g/ → [ç] gente [çente] ‘people’.

The direction taken by lenition seems to be unusually sensitive to the possibilities allowed by the distribution of other sound segments. The change in voice with /g/ before front vowels may be due to the prior existence of /j/ as a phoneme. And [ç] is not the realisation of /x/231: agua [aɣua] ‘water’, gente [xente] ‘people’, junta [xunta] ‘junta’, jefe [xefe] ‘chef’.

The allophonic lenition of /k/ is not equivalent to the diachronic development of Italian or English or Russian where /k/ → [tʃ] before high vowels. Here it shifts to [θ] and hence remains distinct from /tʃ/: caldera [kaldeɾa] ‘kettle’ centro [θentɾo] ‘centre’, chico [tʃiko] ‘boy’.

Another aspect of consonant realisations is that the voicing of /s/ only occurs before voiced obstruents and sonorants but not intervocalically: desde [dezðe] ‘since’, mismo [mizmo] ‘self’, but casa [kasa] ‘house’. A certain degree of overlap is present with /θ/, z. But while this is also a realisation of /k/ its most common occurrence at the end of a word makes for a quasi-complementary distribution with [θ] ← /k/ which occurs prevocalically. It shows the same voicing before obstruents as does /s/.

(31)232





A further aspect of Castilian Spanish lenition is the disappearance of certain fricative consonants word finally.

(32) /d/ → [ð], Ø /V__# Madrid /maðri(ð)/





7. Danish


Danish lenition occupies an intermediate stage between Spanish and Irish showing the final stage, that of vocalisation. The treatment of Danish lenition from which the following facts are drawn, and which is similar to the present study in the generality of its conclusions, is the comprehensive treatment by Hans Basbøll (2005). A comparative description of Danish within the remaining Scandinavian languages is given in Wessén (1979: 71-79).

The first stage of lenition in Danish is the voicing of intervocalic voiceless stops: /p, t, k/ → /b, d, g/. These stops are retained initially where they are considerably more aspirated than in English. Just like initial stress in Irish, Finnish, Czech, etc. this aspiration also has the function of showing word boundaries. [+aspirated] → # ___. It also maximalises the difference between initial and non-initial233 stops. Word-finally stops show the following development: /b, d, g/ → /v, ð, v/ (Jakobson, Fant and Halle 1952: 5). The ambi-dental voiced fricative [ð] represents the end point in a sequence of lenition234 for coronal stops and is followed by zero whereas labial and velar stops usually vocalise and form diphthongs. Velar stops split into a more front stop, vocalising to /i/, and a more back one, resulting in /u/. This can be illustrated diagrammatically as follows.

(33)





This progression can be documented for Danish as follows (only final stages are given).

(34)





This development is comparable to that in Irish where the progression corresponds to that given for Danish where voiced velar stops and labial fricatives were vocalised. Cases with dh in the orthography were shifted from [ð] to [ɣ] and it is this velar element which forms the second part of the diphthong seen in a word like bodhar /baur/ ‘deaf’. On the other hand th, which represented [θ], did not shift to a velar position but disappeared: tuath /tuə/ ‘country’, cliath /kjljiə/ ‘hurdle’. The sequence was probably: /t/ → /θ/ → /h/ → Ø (support for the penultimate /h/ is offered by the lenited form of T in present-day Irish which is also /h/).





8. Baltic Finnic


In Finnish and Estonian, both Baltic-Finnic languages, a change in internal consonant structure has become associated with certain cases of nouns and conjugated forms of verbs. In Finnish the original motivation for consonant gradation (M. Gordon 1998) is still to be seen, the closed short syllable which led to the reduction of the consonants preceding it. In Estonian phonetic attrition has led to the loss of final segments (Lavotha 1973: 19) and this has obscured the phonetic motivation for gradation in this language. It has also meant that with the absence of phonetic conditioning analogy led to the spread of gradation so that it came to affect vowels, e.g. by reducing overlong vowels to long ones. Gradation is a strictly word-internal phenomenon in Baltic Finnic whereas the mutations of Irish are initial while palatalisation and velarisation affect the ends of words (syllable codas). But gradation shows a similar origin to the initial mutation lenition in that it rests on the weakening of the articulation of a segment or segments (Holman 1975: 56-67).





8.1. Finnish


There are four types of gradation in Finnish (Karlsson 1999, Pochtrager 2008), divided into two groups: gradation proper and assimilation.235 These consist of the following processes: 1) simplification of geminates and consonant clusters, 2) voicing and fricativisation, 3) vocalisation. Gradation occurs word-internally in Finnish and is triggered by a closed short syllable which leads to a phonetic reduction of the consonants preceding it. Such a short syllable is typically represented by an inflection, such as the genitive, cf. jalka ‘foot’ : jalan ‘foot.GEN’. The agglutinative suffixes of Finnish are still present and so the process is transparent as opposed to Estonian which, due to the loss of final inflections, has opacified gradation as a synchronic process. The existence of a full inflectional system means that there is no immediate motivation to functionalise gradation in Finnish. 236

Gradation can be analysed as consisting of three steps.

(35)





The first stage, simplification, involves two steps, assimilation and geminate shortening. These steps are the least problematic parts of Finnish consonant gradation and involve the assimilation of a stop to a homorganic sonorant (Karlsson 1999: 28-38) and the shortening of voiceless plosive geminates.

(36)





The second stage of consonant gradation involves the fricativisation of voiceless stops. The actual changes are the following.

(37)





What is attested here is a change which could be analysed as involving two steps – in terms of sonority – namely a shift from voiceless consonant to voiceless fricative and then from the latter to a voiced fricative. However, such a view would rest on the assumption that the language for which this ordering is postulated has a voiceless/voiced distinction across its phoneme inventory. This is not the case in Finnish where the general consonantal pairing factor is length. The behaviour of /k/ in other cases of gradation may furthermore suggest that the shift of the voiceless velar stop was first to a homorganic voiced fricative, i.e. one would have the following sequence in the history of Finnish.

(38) k → ɣ → j / Sonorant __ V [+front, +high]





The steps in this derivation can be used to account for the /k/ to Ø shift (below) and the /k/ to /v/ shift. The surface occurrence of /v/ is in the environment /u_u/. A further shift of /ɣ/ to /v/ can be assumed in the surrounding of a high round back vowel. Given a general velar-labial correlation, which has also been established for Irish (they both belong to the peripheral area in the Jakobsonian sense of ‘grave’), the shift from velar to labial articulation for the intervocalic fricative is understandable. Skousen (1975: 52) sees this as part of a rounding tendency which is present in Finnish, cf. /e/ → /ø/ in the Savo dialect (tekevi → tekövi ‘he does’). The final stage of gradation involves the following changes.

(39)





It is above all the irregularity in these forms which has led to the postulation of underlying forms which underwent certain changes. The initial stage gave rise to unstable fricatives: p, t, k → ß, ð, ɣ. The first of these shifted slightly in articulation from bilabial to labiodental (Holman 1975: 118). The resulting /v/ was stable and remained in the language. The velar fricative had three developments. Firstly, it disappeared between a sonorant and a back vowel.237 Secondly, it shifted to /v/ between high back vowels. And thirdly, it became /j/ between a sonorant and a front vowel. There remains the gradation of /t/ (/t/ → /ð/ → /d/). This is the most problematic as there is no language internal evidence for the provection of fricatives to stops. Skousen (1975: 96) and Collinder (1965: 88) see /d/ as arising from contact with Swedish. Note that the fortition of a dental fricative to /d/ occurred in German (Keller 1978: 173) and in the North Germanic238 languages, for instance, with the second person singular personal pronoun (/θu/ → /du/).





8.2. Estonian


Consonant gradation in Estonian (Raun and Saareste 1965: 22-25) shows a quantitative and a qualitative stage. The first (Lavotha 1973: 18-19) corresponds to a simplification stage in the lenition hierarchy and is regular (as in Finnish) for both consonants and vowels (Holman 1975: 166-181). The second is more complicated and shows the interaction of several factors which together exhibit regularity. The voiced stops show two possibilities of gradation. Gradation in Estonian is purely grammatical so that the occurrence of two theoretically competing stages of lenition (here: a voiced fricative and a null element) in the same phonetic environment is possible.

(40)





When Ø arises the preceding vowel is also lowered: rida ‘row’, rea ‘row.GEN’. The fricativisation of /d/ to /j/ is the same as in Danish (before high vowels). The shift of /g/ to /j/ means that it does not disappear as does /k/, which does not palatalise to /ç/. As in Finnish the sonorants can occur with a following plosive which on gradation simplifies to a geminate though not in all cases. Syllabic structure plays a role here as in Italian and Swedish so that one has the following change:

(41)





where S = /l, r, n/ and O = /d, g/. When O = /b/ it coalesces to a geminate with the homorganic sonorant /m/, /lb/ → /lv/, /rb/ → /rv/, /mb/ → /mm/. Furthermore, /g/ is retained as /j/ when followed by a high vowel.

(42)





where S = /l, r/ and Cg = /g/ and Cj = /j/. The voiceless plosives disappear on gradation. They are not all affected and the forms they occur in may be unexpected.

(43)





9. Nivkh


A further manifestation of lenition is found in Nivkh, a Paleosiberian language (Comrie 1981: 266-271) spoken on part of Sakhalin Island and along a stretch of the Amur river,239 where stops are lenited to fricatives (Jakobson 1971, II: 91) which in terms of articulatory force represents a weakening. Jakobson (1971, II: 89-90, fn.41) notes parallels to Celtic (with reference to Sommerfelt) and to Fula (with reference to Klingenheben). 240

The status of Nivkh is contested and it has no generally recognised relatives.241 Typologically, Nivkh is an incorporating language.242 Nivkh is without a written history and the development of the language from a possibly non-incorporating to the present-day incorporating state cannot be traced. The concern here is with initial mutation in a synchronic sense.

The syntax of Nivkh is ordered according to sentences and so-called sections (Jakobson 1971, II: 78; JaNSSR 1968: 429-430). A syntactic section is a sequence of morphemes before and after which a pause may occur. The morphemes within such a sequence generally do not occur in isolation, a fact which suggests the use of the term ‘incorporation’. The morphemes within a sequence can vary according to the nature of the initial consonant, i.e. Nivkh mutation only affects consonants. Before looking at this system in Nivkh it is necessary to consider the sound system.

The first basic distinction is between manner and place of articulation. With the latter Nivkh distinguishes four places: labial, dental, palatal, velar-uvular (Nedjalkov and Otaina 2013: 3). The last two have been linked because velars have been recently introduced to Nivkh via loanwords. Jakobson (1971, II: 85) however seeks to distinguish the velar from the uvular points of articulation because of the assimilation of these loans. For a system of initial mutation the points of articulation at a language’s disposal are not relevant. There do not seem to be any languages which have a consistent and extensive system of alternation of points of articulation in their synchronic systems such as /p, f, v/ → /t, s, z/ → /k, x, ɣ/ but there may be changes in point of articulation accompanying a change in manner, recall Irish D to ɣ. Changes such as S to /h/ can be explained within the possibilities of alternation which the language offers for certain inputs, in this case to the process of lenition.

In Nivkh there are voiced and voiceless fricatives while stops show a threefold division as voiceless aspirated,243 voiceless unaspirated and voiced. However, the status of voiced stops is controversial because Nivkh lacks roots with initial voiced stops and so the latter can be regarded as allophones of unaspirated stops (Hidetoshi Shiraishi personal communication), hence the square brackets used in (44) below. Gruzdeva (1998: 11) also maintains that voiced stops do not occur initially in lexical citations forms in all dialects with the possible exception of South Sakhalin.

To describe the Nivkh alternations in terms of classes and not of individual sounds the following notation for the various manners of articulation is used.

(44) voiceless aspirated stops Ph

voiceless non-aspirated Pvl

[voiced plosives Pvd]

voiceless fricatives Fvl

voiced fricatives Fvd





The alternation Fvl ~ Fvd applies to /r/ sounds as well as Nivkh has a voiceless (fricative) /r/ : //.

The five points of articulation and the five manners of articulation result in the following matrix.244 In accordance with the practice also used for Atlantic-Congo languages in the Niger-Congo phylum (see below), the manners of articulation have been marked with a Roman numeral. These series can also be referred to as ‘grades’.

(45)





The difference between //, /s/ and /r/, /z/ is not just one of place of articulation but also of manner. But on the strength of the relations of //, /r/ and /s/, /z/ to the other members of the horizontal group, in which they are placed within the system of morphophonemic alternations, they can be regarded as manifesting differences in place of articulation and the manner difference between sonorants and fricatives can be disregarded.

The regularity of these patterns is noticeable when compared with Irish which shows more irregularity in this area than Nivkh. Variation in mutational patterns is, however, abundantly present among the Atlantic-Congo languages, see below. The historical development of mutation in Nivkh is a case of the development from phonetic to morphonological conditioning. There are remnants of the former still. For example, after nasals aspirated plosives lose their aspiration and this also applies across morpheme boundaries, in which case it resembles the Irish mutation ‘nasalisation’ in that there is a movement towards voicing, even if this is not reached. The phonetic realisations show the next step where the nasal is not pronounced (in the Amur dialect, Gruzdeva 1998: 11) leaving de-aspiration of the initial segment of the following morpheme as its reflex: /oʁlaN - tho - ra/ [oʁlatora]245 ‘carried the child’ (Savel’jeva and Taksami 1970: 322).

Two further apparent cases of phonetic conditioning can be observed. The first is the lack of two fricatives at morpheme boundaries or in morphologically complex forms. Nivkh allows sequences of two fricatives within a single morpheme (see the name of the language as an example), but it appears to disfavour them across morpheme boundaries or at a prosodic pause (Shiraishi 2006: 96). This can be seen with morphemes which have a stop-initial and a fricative-initial variant. The allative inflection and the plural thematic suffix are such instances. The stop-initial variant is found after fricatives (and nasals) while the fricative-initial variant occurs after stops, liquids and vowels. Consider the following forms which involve the allative inflection /tox/ ‘to’.

(46)246





This would seem to apply generally with the exception of the instrumental/ modal suffix /-s/ and the locative /-f/ which are immutable.

A further alternation is seen in intervocalic voicing, cf. /njivɣ-avi-/ ‘to marry a Nivkh’ but /njivx/ ‘Nivkh’, i.e. there is voicing in a voiced environment as there is in the following example where the prefix creates a voiced context: /tjxəf/ ‘bear’ /phizɣəf/ ‘one’s own bear’ (/phi/ = reflexive pronoun).

Before looking at its grammatical status, the source of such phonetic conditioning should be considered. It can be assumed to begin with that plosives and fricatives existed in medial and final position (although Jakobson 1971, II: 95) gives examples of dialectal variants such as /njetj/ ~ /njes/ ‘a plank’; /atjik/ ~ /asik/ ‘grandmother’ to suggest that the distinction was a matter of allophony) and that the initial position of morphemes did not show this distinction. But as a whole the distinction was operative, cf. pairs like /pək/ ‘cuckoo’ versus /pəx/ ‘colour’. However, in medial position there was no distinction between fortis and lenis as opposed to the situation with initial consonants which did contrast in this parameter. Jakobson (1971, II: 94-95) compares this position with that in Danish where there are strong and weak obstruents, such as the voiceless /t/ and the voiced /d/. The weak obstruent, e.g. /d/, in the strong position is however equivalent to the strong one in the weak (medial) position, the weak one in this latter position being /ð/.

(47) Phonological strength and position in Danish

Initial Medial

Strong t d

Weak d ð





A similar overlap existed in Nivkh where fortis and lenis occurred initially but in medial position only lenis was found, that is the weak obstruent in the strong position overlapped with the strong one in the weak position, for example /kh/ + /k/ initially, but medially only /k/ + /x/.

Present-day initial fricatives result from preposed vocalic elements which were later lost leaving plosives and fricatives contrasting. This loss of initial vowels would seem to be due to the stressing of the incorporated sequence which was weak initially. It would also explain the clitic nature of prefixes and their incorporation into following root morphemes (noted in Jakobson 1971, II: 87-88) as in /u-opu-/ ‘to gather together’ (/u/ = ‘together’) → /wopu-/ → /vopu/ or /nji/ ‘my’, /nj/ as in /nj-rəf/ ‘my house’.

Consider also the case of transitivity which is expressed by the prepositive use of at least the indefinite object /i-/ ‘something’. This shifted an initial plosive to medial position and fricativised it. Hence the absolute condition in present-day Nivkh that transitive verbs always begin with a fricative (Jakobson 1971, II: 86): /tjhe-/ ‘to get dry’, /se-u-/ ‘to dry (something)’. Observe also such pairs as the following where the transitive ~ intransitive relation has been obscured by semantic shift: /pəks/ ‘to disappear’, /vəks/ ‘to throw’.

The synchronic set of morphonological alternations does not interact randomly with the grades presented in (45) above. Only certain alternations can occur. The determining factor for an alternation is the final consonant of the preceding morpheme. The alternations which have been touched upon already can be formulated as follows.

(48)247





These rules would appear to be mirror image rules motivated by an original and partially viable double fricative prohibition. This prohibition must be qualified because there are grammatically conditioned exceptions to it. For example, when two nominal stem morphemes combine to give a compound the prohibition does not apply: // ‘house-door’. The feature [+sonorant] in Nivkh applies to nasals and liquids only because r-sounds are morphonologically fricatives.

(49)248





These realisations can be accounted for by a two-part constraint on the realisation of obstruents in post-sonorant position:

(50) An obstruent following a fricative is realised as a non-fricative

An unaspirated stop following a voiced segment is progressively voiced





What part of the constraint applies depends on the input, i.e. fricatives are fortified to stops and input stops are voiced. This phonological behaviour is similar to nasalisation in Irish which has a different result depending on the type of input, i.e. voiceless stops are voiced and voiced ones are nasalised.

It is obvious that in Nivkh (as in Irish) the grammatical conditioning of mutations is well-established. Thus the progressive voicing rule is subject to certain grammatical conditions. For instance, it applies to prepositive pronominal morphemes but not to prepositive nominal morphemes: /smo/ ‘like’, /e-zmo/ ‘to like something’, but: /ma-smo/ ‘to like dried fish’. In general, however, the initial segment of root morphemes (nominal or verbal stems) is free of such conditioning as it is in absolute initial position (though verbs and some nouns are not always in this position when in syntactic sequences because of the SOV verb order). What is meant here is that they have a base form which is independent of mutation whereas the various suffixes are clitic in nature and consist of forms embedded in contexts.

The freedom of root morphemes is exploited to indicate lexical class, i.e. mutation in absolute initial position has lexical value, e.g. /af/ ‘to hook something’, /thaf/ ‘the hook’, /xes/ ‘to tell’, /khes/ ‘information’. This situation is different from Irish which does not use mutation to indicate a lexical class but rather suffixation, e.g. beannaigh /bjanɣ/ + /ə/ ‘bless’; beannacht /bjanɣ/ + /əxt/ ‘blessing’.

In general the functional load of mutation is far higher in Nivkh than in Irish. For example, it serves to identify subject and object and to separate them. When a noun preceding a transitive verb causes the latter’s initial segment to be realised as a fricative then it is the subject of the verb; if it causes this to be a plosive then it is the object and has another noun preceding it in turn, cf. /tjxəf-o-ra/ ‘the bear carried (something)’ versus /tjəŋ-tjxəf-tho-ra/ ‘you-PL carried the bear’.

Finally, Nivkh shows definite parallels to Irish in the area of loanwords. When a language with mutations adopts loanwords one can distinguish two types of borrowing: (1) loanwords which have sound substitution in accordance with the segment inventory of the borrowing language and (2) loanwords which take part fully in the system of mutations. Both of these situations apply to Nivkh. For instance, with borrowings from Nanay (a member of the South-East Tungusic group of languages), sound substitution applies. To give the example of the best-known Nanay word: /saman/ → Nivkh /tjham/ ‘shaman’ (Jakobson 1971, II: 93). The mutation of loanwords can be seen in a case like /siŋgərə/ ‘to torture’ → /tjxəɟ-tjhəŋr-u-/ ‘to torture a bear’. The change here is from fortis fricative (base form of the verb) to fortis plosive when the direct object precedes.

As with Irish, however, Nivkh has tended to lapse on applying sound substitution and mutation to recent loans. For example, in Irish affricates –which are extraneous to its sound system – are to be found with recent English loanwords such as chips, jeep, etc. In Nivkh later borrowings with initial fricatives in the donor language retain these after being borrowed although native Nivkh nouns have initial plosives: Nanay /seata/ → Nivkh /seta/ ‘sugar’.





10. Fula


Fula, a language spoken in parts of West Africa, is a further case of a language which shows initial consonant mutation. Fula249 is generally regarded as belonging to the Atlantic-Congo group250 of the Niger-Congo phylum (Williamson and Blench 2000) and on a par with other such languages such as Serer and Wolof. Fula has been the subject of much research, some of it quite speculative (for example Mukarovsky (1963) who attempts to link it up with Berber251, Mukarovsky 1963: 98) and Basque (Mukarovsky 1963: 142), see also Meinhof (1932, 1947: 53) who postulates Hamitic origins. Early descriptive work was done by Klingenheben, spanning nearly four decades (Klingenheben 1924, 1927, 1941, 1963). Westermann (1909) and Taylor (1953 [1921]) are other early monographs. More recent studies are Arnott (1970), Skousen (1972), J. D. Sapir (1971) and Paradis (1992).

From the point of view of phonology, Fula poses difficulties not encountered with Nivkh, for instance. For one the term ‘Fula’ covers a dialect continuum which stretches from North Senegal to Central Guinea with at least three pockets of speakers spread as far east as Western Sudan (J. D. Sapir 1971: 52). It is spoken by well over 20 million people in all as opposed to the very small numbers of Nivkh. Fula is closely related to Serer and less closely related to Wolof (Nikiforova 1981: 20-35).

Initial mutation in Fula (Lieber 1983, 1984) occurs with verbs and nouns and for the purpose of illustration the latter will be considered here. Nouns in Fula fall into certain classes which are determined (1) by characteristic suffixes and (2) by initial consonant mutation. An extensive classification of these classes is given in Klingenheben (1924) with detailed phonetic descriptions (1924: 191). He divides the prefix-classes, i.e. mutations, according to distribution across singular and plural so that he arrives at five classes (1924: 194 + 202-203) whereas later authors simply refer to the three grades and content themselves with the statement that a given noun may not have the same grade in the singular and plural.

The classification of the nouns is also done by means of semantic features such [animate], [abstract], [augmentative], [diminutive], etc., there being one or more suffixes corresponding to a feature or combination of features. The number of classes varies greatly from dialect to dialect hence the figures in the relevant literature diverge: Greenberg (1963: 25) has sixteen classes for the singular and six for the plural; Westermann and Bryan (1952: 25) have 28 classes for the Fula dialects as a whole. There are three mutation series or grades as follows.

(51) I : fricative

II : plosive

III : prenasal252



/rawa-ndu/ ‘dog’

/rawa/ root with initial fricative253

/ndu/ class suffix

/dawa ɗi/ ‘dogs’

/dawa/ root with initial stop

/ɗi/ class suffix





Each of the 22 classes has one of the grades. The singular and plural of a noun do not necessarily belong to the same class and hence the initial consonant of the root is not necessarily the same either. This may nonetheless be the case if (i) the singular and plural do in fact belong to the same class, (ii) if the different classes to which they belong require the same mutation grade or (iii) the initial consonant in the singular and plural is immutable. Only with one of the suffixes (the /-ko/ suffix, Westermann and Bryan 1952: 26) is more that one grade possible, in fact all three grades occur here.

The phonetic motivation for mutation is not evident and it is grammatically conditioned by the appearance of an element (a suffix) which is phonetically unconnected with it. Furthermore, the phonetic shape of the suffix bears no relation to the mutation grade required, specifically the initial consonant of the suffix is not that of the mutation grade required, it is either a stop or a nasal; this means that for one grade, the fricative grade I, the characteristic sound for this grade can never be that at the beginning of the suffix.

If one looks at the phonetic manifestation of the three grades in Fula, or any of the other Atlantic-Congo languages254 with initial mutations (Ternes 1990: 5-10), then it is obvious that the forms found can be related to more regular series if rules of realisation are taken into account or certain plausible shifts are postulated, see Ladefoged (1968: 45) for an overview of the phonological contrasts. It would seem appropriate to posit base forms for the system of mutation and to derive other forms from these bases. However, J. D. Sapir (1971:70) doubts the appropriateness of this. The question of what serves as such a base is not as easily settled as in Irish or even Nivkh. All three mutational grades occur in citation forms. It is not a matter of decontextualising a form where possible, as in Nivkh, or of reverting to the nominative singular or the present indicative, as in Irish. One possible method is to look at the set of noun classes and take the grade which occurs most frequently as the basic one. The numerical distribution of mutations among suffixes is as follows, determined on the basis of Westermann and Bryan’s twenty eight class system with one suffix without a grade specification (Westermann and Bryan 1952: 25-26).

(52)





According to this the fricative grade is the least well represented. The stop grade appears in fact to be even more basic if one bears in mind that grade III is that of pre-nasalised stops so that the class of stops is the input to this grade, leaving a proportion of 21: 6 in favour of a plosive base form. In view of this, consider the phonetic manifestation of the grades in Fula.

(53)255, 256





The consonants which do not mutate fall into two classes: sonorants and implosives. Ladefoged (1968: 45) transcribes the latter as preglottalised, i.e. /?b/, /?d/, etc. Their exact phonetic nature is irrelevant here as they are not involved in the mutation system. Westermann and Ward (1933: §282) also term these implosives. All authors agree at least that there is a separate glottal stop acting as an independent segment in Fula (Westermann and Ward 1933: 162).

In Fula, as in Nivkh, the sonorants cover only nasals and liquids as /r/ is considered a fricative and takes part in mutation. /t/ is also immutable but in various Eastern Senegal languages, such as Basari and Bedik, it does mutate (with /s/257 in grade I) and in Konyagi, Biafada and Kasanga it does so with /r/, note that these latter three languages do not have /d/. /h/ has arisen from /x/ so that the series with /k/ is regular in its fricative, while the /b/ and /g/ series show the development of the fricatives /ß/ and /ɣ/ into glides as /w/; /w/ and /j/ have complementary distribution before back and front vowels respectively. /s/ corresponds with palatal stops, not only in Fula.

Prenasalisation258 in Fula only affects voiced stops. This would seem to be similar to the position in Irish where nasalisation is a single step process. As the prenasalisation of /k/ for example would involve two feature changes – [-voice] to [+voice] and [-nasal] to [+nasal] – there is no prenasalisation of voiceless stops.259 As opposed to Irish, however, prenasalisation would seem to involve either one step to nasalisation or no step, i.e. /k/ does not change to /g/ as one of the steps on the way to nasalisation as it does in Irish. The same notion of change of a single feature on mutation applies to grade I as well. Voiceless stops, except immutable /t/ in Fula, never appear in this grade. What this means is that the single step of [-cont] to [+cont] is made by voiceless stops. But a number of voiced stops are also to be found in grade I. At first sight this may seem unexpected. However, if the system in Atlantic-Congo languages is seen in a broader perspective then it can be shown that the occurrence of voiced plosives in grade I is a regularity in itself.

Assuming that grade II, the stop grade, is the basic one, then grade I can be seen as the lenition grade and grade III as the nasalisation grade. The latter does not include voicing of voiced stops, perhaps for the language-internal reason that this is part of grade I. The essential nature of the grades corresponds to the position in Irish with zero mutation, lenition and nasalisation. In Fula, however, voicing of voiceless stops is part of lenition. The voiced stops only occur as variants of glides (from voiced fricatives) in the first grade. Looking now at the closely related language Serer: 260 one finds that the situation with the grades is in full accordance with the following lenition hierarchy (presented in the discussion of Finnish above).

(54)





Stages 2a and 2b above may overlap, e.g. voiceless stops may become voiceless fricatives directly (without becoming voiced stops first). For both Serer and Fula Stage 1 in the above hierarchy is represented by the grade II with grade I the outcome of Stage 2b above. Grade III is a different mutation – nasalisation – and is not part of the lenition hierarchy. When considering both these languages it should be borne in mind that implosives, sonorants and the glide /j/ are immutable.

(55) Initial mutation in Serer





Here nasalisation is one or two steps (contrast Fula above), that is, both voiced stops and voiceless stops nasalise to clusters of nasal and voiced stop.261 That the voiceless stops become voiced here on nasalisation (change of two features) may be favoured by the possibility of voicing in the language in general (cf. the voicing of voiceless stops in the first grade) whereas this is not found in Fula (except for the less usual voiced stop variants in grade I). The change from grade II to I in Serer shows the two possibilities of Stage 2a and 2b in the above hierarchy.

(56)





The only alternation not accounted for here is /t/ ~ /r/. Here phonological considerations are decisive. In Serer (as in Atlantic-Congo in general) alveolar stops alternate with /r/ sounds (as they do in Nivkh) and palatals alternate with /s/. This situation implies that phonologically /r/ is a fricative, the voiced equivalent of /s/.

Consider finally two conditions which apply to mutation globally in Northern Atlantic-Congo and the Eastern Senegal group as well (J. D. Sapir 1971: 66): (1) geminates of all types never appear in grade I and are never immutable, (2) voiceless geminates and nasal geminates appear in grade III only as a repeat of themselves (J. D. Sapir 1971: 66).

The second condition is firm evidence for the contention that grade II is basic. Looking at the above lenition hierarchy again one sees that geminates are the first stage on a lenition hierarchy, that is, they result from assimilating homorganic consonants to one manner of articulation as in Finnish gradation. If they are found in the base form then on mutation they must change along the scale of lenition. This they do and simplify to single stops for grade I (the lenition grade) as in the following.

(57) Biafada262 geminate simplification





The reflexive nature of the second condition – geminates only appear as a repetition of themselves – is explained quite simply. Firstly, there are Atlantic-Congo languages where nasals can alternate. Then the alternation is simply: nongeminate ~ geminate as in the following.

(58) Biafada (and reconstructed Pajade)





As grade II is basic, grade I represents lenition (here: geminate simplification). Grade III [nasalisation] applies vacuously as the base grade already consists of nasals. Secondly, there are languages (such as Fula) where nasalisation is restricted to one feature change. If such languages also have geminates (as Pajade for example) then the voiceless geminates will appear as such in the nasalisation grade as they cannot undergo a two feature change.

Recapitulating the system of mutation in Atlantic-Congo and Irish it will be seen that what one is dealing with is an alternation of base forms in one of two directions.

(59) nasalisation ← base form → lenition





There are grounds for regarding the two mutations as converses of each other. Not only that, but if one views the lenition hierarchy once more then one notices that nasalisation for Atlantic-Congo (and diachronically for Irish) is a reverse of the initial stage of the lenition hierarchy.

(60) Nasalisation

Ci (Ci) → Cj (Ci)

where Ci is a stop and Cj is a homorganic nasal





On this basis one can formulate the following characterisation of mutation

(61) Morphonologically lenition is a weakening, and nasalisation is a strengthening of the base form.





It should be stressed that this applies on a morphonological level because phonetically the position is somewhat different. Voicing in Irish is phonetically lenition but morphonologically it is nasalisation. However, the other results of morphonological nasalisation, namely nasals, are phonetically strengthening as nasals are relatively stable elements which do not lenite to another set of consonants, although they may under circumstances disappear through being absorbed by a preceding vowel and by the subsequent shift of nasal vowel to oral vowel.





11. Southern Paiute


The Paiute language, which divides into the quite different branches Northern and Southern Paiute, is a member of the Numic branch of the Uto-Aztec language family in the USA. It is actually a continuum which stretches from south-eastern California to Colorado with a concentration in the Colorado river basin and North Arizona. Individual dialects are Chemehuevi (M. Press 1975, 1979) as well as Southern Paiute (of which San Juan and Kaibab are subdialects) and Ute proper (Mithun 1999: 541-542).

A limited amount of research is available on Southern Paiute beginning with discussions of its grammar by Edward Sapir in (1930), a reconsideration of some of his claims in Harms (1966) and more recently the two dictionaries by Charney (1996) and Givón (1979) and the reference grammar by Givón (1980). Oberly (2004, 2008) are the most recent studies recording the language of the remaining speakers (less than 100). More general information about this ethnic group is available in Jefferson, Delaney and Thompson (1972).263

The most substantial discussion of morphonology can be found in E. Sapir (1930) who considers the changes triggered by affixes on the stems to which they are attached (see also the comments in E. Sapir 1949 [1933]: 49-52). The effects of such affixes can be classified as fricativisation, gemination and prenasalisation, basically an embryonic system of mutation which has four different manifestations (three changes and zero mutation).

Table 2. Consonant mutations in Southern Paiute after E. Sapir





For example, the absolutive suffix -pi appears in different forms, according to which noun stem it is suffixed to: movi-ppi ‘nose’, sappi-vi ‘belly’, aŋo-mpi ‘tongue’.264

Although the amount of reliable information for Southern Paiute is limited, what has been recorded shows a number of segmental changes triggered by internal sandhi which demonstrate (i) a characteristic weakening mutation, here fricativisation and (ii) a pair of strengthening mutations, gemination and prenasalisation., very similar to that of Old Irish out of which the present-day Irish system evolved.





12. Central Vanuatu languages


Some languages of central Vanuatu, which belong to the Oceanic branch of Austronesian,265 display fairly regular patterns of mutation for the initial segments of verb roots as shown in the study by Crowley (1991). Quoting Schütz (1968), he makes a distinction between primary and secondary consonants (the former are historically prior, hence the label ‘primary’) as follows (Crowley 1991: 180).

(62) Root-initial alternations in Nakanamanga (north Efate, Vanuatu) Primary Secondary

v- p-

w- pw

k- ŋ-

r- t-





The primary forms occur after the preverbal particles pwa ‘imperative’, ŋa ‘future, subjunctive’ and pe ‘conditional’ (along with other grammatical contexts).

(63)





In the Näti language (Crowley 1998) in the south-west interior of Malakula,266 the second largest island in Vanuatu, the secondary forms show regular nasalisation with a change from obstruent to nasal + stop (Crowley 1991: 198).

(64) Root-initial alternations in Näti (Malakula, Vanuatu) Primary Secondary
