Mutation patterns for root-initial segments among verbs can be quite complex with South Ambrym (or Taveak, closely related to Paamese) showing nine classes of verb depending on initial mutation (Crowley 1991: 185).267

(65) South Ambrym verb classes by initial mutation





An example of a verb from Class I would be tis ‘write’ and one from Class IX would be tetep ‘swollen’.

(66) a. i-tis Ø-dis

3sg:dist-write 3sg:real-write

‘he/she will write’ ‘he/she writes/wrote’

b. i-tetep mi-ttep

3sg:dist-swollen 3sg:real-swollen

‘it will be swollen’ ‘it is/was swollen’





Even these simple examples show that the mutations are established and largely regular but they are not solely responsible for the indication of grammatical categories as the triggering particles are still present and phonetically distinguished. Compared to the Celtic situation, Central Vanuatu languages show an embryonic mutational system, much like Tuscan Italian, which in principle has sufficient distinct phonetic realisations to indicate the grammatical categories of the language, but which are not the sole phonetic exponents of these categories. For this to happen the triggering particles would have to become phonetically blurred and lose their distinctiveness thereby possibly triggering reanalysis with a future generation of learners during first language acquisition.





13. Conclusion


The languages and dialects considered in this section all show phrase level sandhi phenomena which established themselves in largely regular patterns as alterations to the initial consonants of lexical stems, i.e. as initial mutations. In some cases, such as Nivkh and Fula, these alterations became indicators of grammatical categories because alternating sets of sounds attained grammatical significance.

Initial mutation first appears in languages as a low-level phenomenon triggered by clitic elements attaching themselves prosodically to lexical stems and altering their initial consonants in the process. At the beginning this would have had no effect on the grammar of a language, its categories being realised by established means, e.g. inflectional suffixes as with the early attested languages of Indo-European. However, if the inflections become phonetically blurred and (near-)homophones arise or endings are lost entirely then later generations of first-language learners may come to re-interpret the low-level mutations as systemic given that the inflections have lost their phonetic profile and prominence. In fact these processes are likely to run hand in hand: the blurring and loss of inflections is the same phonetic process which leads to the sandhi changes to stem-initial consonants which later become systemic.

To move from an inflectional language type to one based on initial mutation is a major step in typological terms and the reanalysis required by first language learners has only taken place in a few instances, notably in the Celtic languages and some of those discussed in the above sections, such as Nivkh and Fula.

When initial mutations become the dominant means of indicating grammatical categories in a language there are usually three distinctions, a system with two distinctions, for instance no change and initial fricativisation, would not seem to trigger typological re-orientation of the kind the Celtic languages experienced. It should be pointed out, however, that a three-way distinction is by no means a sufficient condition for functionalisation, cf. Tuscan Italian and Canary Spanish, discussed above, which retain their largely inflectional character despite the phonetic mutations they show.





13.1. Types of mutation


When reviewing the languages and dialects which show these alterations several generalisations can be made. The first is that initial mutations arise as low-level phonetic phenomena on the level of the syntactic phrase and that they can change stem-initial consonants in one of two ways (Blevins 2006: 144-147).

(67)





In phonological terms all initial mutations can be interpreted as moving in a certain direction, i.e. the stem-initial consonant is either weakened or strengthened. This results in three possible configurations for initial consonants as show in the following.

(68)





Depending on the phonology of a language there may be more than three mutations, but this is generally due to a subdivision of one of the above types, e.g. a language may have two kinds of weakening, e.g. fricativisation and voicing, as in Welsh.

The output of a mutation is generally distinct from the input of a further mutation to avoid confusion about whether a certain point in a grammatical paradigm has mutation or not. Furthermore, no grammatical category involves the application of two mutations at once, e.g. a noun case or a verb tense would involve either the weak mutation or the strong mutation but not both simultaneously (though there may be an additional ‘mixed mutation’ as in Breton).





13.2. Weak and strong mutation


The configurations for initial consonants involve manner of articulation and not place of articulation, with one or two caveats, see following section. The phonetic exponence of initial mutation shows typical modifications as follows.

(69)





When applied to individual segments, certain specific manifestations recur. Voiceless stops can become voiced or fricativise. Voicing does not apply to input voiced segments so weak mutation does not apply though voiced input is common for the strong mutation, cf. the voiced stops in Irish which can undergo nasalisation.





13.2.1. Exponence of weak mutation


Fricatives can undergo the weak mutation as fricativisation, but not in a phonetic sense. The manifestation of the weak mutation with fricatives can be (i) zero or (ii) a shift in articulation. The latter involves the removal of the oral gesture, so that a glottal fricative remains as the output of fricativisation as in Modern Irish.

(70) weak mutation (fricativisation in Irish) /f/ → Ø

/s/ → /h/





Zero as the output of initial mutation has a special status. It is most likely to have arisen due to diachronic developments and not to have been part of embryonic mutation when the system was establishing itself in a language. For instance, /g/ mutates to Ø in both Welsh and Breton as these languages no longer have /ɣ/. The mutation of /f/ to Ø in Irish is different: it has this form because the fricativisation of /s/ results in /h/ (as does the fricativisation of /t/).

Simplification as a manifestation of weak mutation can involve various changes. A common shift is degemination as when two consonants are reduced to one, /kk/ → /k/. There may also be cluster simplification as when /gw/ mutates to /w/ in Breton.





13. 2.2. Exponence of strong mutation


Nasalisation is the prefixation of a nasal to a stop or the replacement of the latter by a nasal. Varieties of a language or related languages often differ in whether they show prenasalisation or nasal replacement of an input stop.

Vowel-initial stems do not show the weak mutation but for the strong mutation there may be a prefixed nasal, as in Irish so that the application of this mutation is visible for such stems, e.g. a n-athair /ə nɣahərj/ ‘their father’.

Gemination as an exponent of the strong mutation obviously only applies to those languages which have length distinctions among consonants. A corollary of this is that, should a language lose consonantal length differences, then gemination ceases to be a manifestation of strong mutation and usually remains as zero mutation (no change to stem-initial consonant). In Old Irish geminates did exist and there was a gemination mutation triggered by the feminine possessive pronoun, cf. Old Irish ammuntar ‘her people’ (← a + muntar) (Thurneysen 1909: 82-85, 151). With the loss of geminates the feminine possessive pronoun became associated with zero mutation (no change to stem-initial consonant), cf. Modern Irish a muintir ‘her people’ .

Provection is strengthening inasmuch as segments become less sonorous, e.g. a voiced stop becomes voiceless or a fricative becomes a plosive. The origin of such changes generally lies in assimilation, though it may well be that the triggering element is not present anymore as with provection in Breton.





13.2.3. Two step and mixed mutation


Some mutations involve the application of more than one subtype, e.g. the ‘mixed mutation’ of Breton is realised by lenition and provection together, cf. oh vlejal [fle:zəl] ‘shouting’ (from blejal, ‘to shout’) discussed in section 2. above.

A two step mutation is somewhat different in that there is an end point but whether it is reached depends on the nature of the input which can only have one feature altered on mutation. Nasalisation in Modern Irish provides a good example of this.

(71) two-step mutation (nasalisation in Irish)





A nasalised segment can have [voice] or [nasal] changed to a positive value, but not the two at once. Hence if the input is [-voice] then nasalisation changes this to [+voice] but [nasal] remains negative, i.e. a word like teach /tjax/ cannot nasalise to *a nteach /ə njax/.





13.3. Diachronic developments


Initial mutation is primarily a change in manner of articulation. The place of articulation is not affected in the beginning, the only generalised exception to this being the shift of sibilants to glottal fricatives, i.e. the remove of the oral gesture from these segments, a common development in many languages, e.g. Irish, Greek, Spanish, etc. with /s/ → /h/.

To have become established in the beginning initial mutations must have been regular, this regularity feeding into the typological reanalysis of mutations as grammatically significant with language learners. Equally, a large degree of irregularity would seem necessary for the system to be dismantled again with alternative means for indicating grammatical categories being required for reanalysis to be triggered. The inertia in the system is probably why it is has survived so long in languages such as Breton and Irish which have experienced phonetic shifts which in principle undermine the regularity and predictability of initial mutations. Foremost among these changes in Irish is (i) the velar shift which came to affect voiced coronal stops in Middle Irish, (ii) the loss of interdental fricatives which meant that the output of leniting sibilants and voiceless coronal stops became the same, a glottal fricative /h/.

(72) Velar shift in Middle Irish: D + Lenition → ɣ

dán /dɑ:nɣ/ a dán /ə ɣɑ:nɣ/

‘poem’ ‘his poem’

S + T syncretism on lenition

súil /su:lj/ a shúil /ə hu:lj/

‘eye’ ‘his eye’

tuath /tuə/ a thuath /ə huə/

‘country’ ‘his country’





These changes, and the demise of consonant gemination after Old Irish, altered the system of initial mutations, but the grammatical categories where these were to be found remained stable and the homophony triggered by the developments just outlined was accommodated within the language, largely through contextualisation, allowing the mutations to continue as the primary instrument for the expression of grammatical distinctions in Irish.
